<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Architecture</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Product and process of planning, designing and constructing buildings and other structures</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the profession, see <a href="/wiki/Architect" title="Architect">Architect</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Architecture_(disambiguation)" title="Architecture (disambiguation)">Architecture (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:View_of_Santa_Maria_del_Fiore_in_Florence.jpg"><img alt="View of Florence showing the dome, which dominates everything around it. It is octagonal in plan and ovoid in section. It has wide ribs rising to the apex with red tiles in between and a marble lantern on top." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4912" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/View_of_Santa_Maria_del_Fiore_in_Florence.jpg/320px-View_of_Santa_Maria_del_Fiore_in_Florence.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/View_of_Santa_Maria_del_Fiore_in_Florence.jpg/480px-View_of_Santa_Maria_del_Fiore_in_Florence.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/View_of_Santa_Maria_del_Fiore_in_Florence.jpg/640px-View_of_Santa_Maria_del_Fiore_in_Florence.jpg 2x" width="320"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:View_of_Santa_Maria_del_Fiore_in_Florence.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In adding the dome to the  <a href="/wiki/Florence_Cathedral" title="Florence Cathedral">Florence Cathedral</a> (<a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>) in the early 15th century, the architect <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Brunelleschi" title="Filippo Brunelleschi">Filippo Brunelleschi</a> not only transformed the building and the city, but also the role and status of the <a href="/wiki/Architect" title="Architect">architect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><b>Architecture</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> <i> <i lang="la"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/architectura#Latin" title="wikt:architectura">architectura</a></i></i>; from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <i> </i><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%80%CF%81%CF%87%CE%B9%CF%84%CE%AD%CE%BA%CF%84%CF%89%CE%BD#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:ἀρχιτέκτων">ἀρχιτέκτων</a></span><i> (<span lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i>arkhitéktōn</i></span>)</i> 'architect'; from <i> </i><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%80%CF%81%CF%87%CE%B9-#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:ἀρχι-">ἀρχι-</a></span><i> (<span lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i>arkhi-</i></span>)</i> 'chief', and <i> </i><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%84%CE%AD%CE%BA%CF%84%CF%89%CE%BD#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:τέκτων">τέκτων</a></span><i> (<span lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i>téktōn</i></span>)</i> 'creator') is the art and technique of designing and building, as distinguished from the skills associated with construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> It is both the process and the product of sketching, conceiving,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Planning" title="Planning">planning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Design" title="Design">designing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Construction" title="Construction">constructing</a> <a href="/wiki/Building" title="Building">buildings</a> or other <a href="/wiki/Structure" title="Structure">structures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Architectural works, in the material form of buildings, are often perceived as cultural symbols and as <a href="/wiki/Work_of_art" title="Work of art">works of art</a>. Historical civilizations are often identified with their surviving architectural achievements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The practice, which began in the <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistoric era</a>, has been used as a way of expressing <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a> for civilizations on all seven <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> For this reason, architecture is considered to be a form of <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>. Texts on architecture have been written since ancient times. The earliest surviving text on <a href="/wiki/Architectural_theory" title="Architectural theory">architectural theories</a> is the 1st century AD treatise <i><a href="/wiki/De_architectura" title="De architectura">De architectura</a></i> by the Roman architect <a href="/wiki/Vitruvius" title="Vitruvius">Vitruvius</a>, according to whom a good building embodies <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">firmitas, utilitas</i></span>, and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">venustas</i></span> (durability, utility, and beauty). Centuries later, <a href="/wiki/Leon_Battista_Alberti" title="Leon Battista Alberti">Leon Battista Alberti</a> developed his ideas further, seeing beauty as an objective quality of buildings to be found in their proportions. <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Vasari" title="Giorgio Vasari">Giorgio Vasari</a> wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Lives_of_the_Most_Excellent_Painters,_Sculptors,_and_Architects" title="Lives of the Most Excellent Painters, Sculptors, and Architects">Lives of the Most Excellent Painters, Sculptors, and Architects</a></i> and put forward the idea of style in the Western arts in the 16th century. In the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Sullivan" title="Louis Sullivan">Louis Sullivan</a> declared that "<a href="/wiki/Form_follows_function" title="Form follows function">form follows function</a>". "Function" began to replace the classical "utility" and was understood to include not only practical but also aesthetic, psychological and cultural dimensions. The idea of <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_architecture" title="Sustainable architecture">sustainable architecture</a> was introduced in the late 20th century.
</p><p>Architecture began as rural, oral <a href="/wiki/Vernacular_architecture" title="Vernacular architecture">vernacular architecture</a> that developed from trial and error to successful replication. Ancient urban architecture was preoccupied with building religious structures and buildings symbolizing the political power of rulers until Greek and Roman architecture shifted focus to civic virtues. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_architecture" title="Indian architecture">Indian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_architecture" title="Chinese architecture">Chinese architecture</a> influenced forms all over Asia and <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_architecture" title="Buddhist architecture">Buddhist architecture</a> in particular took diverse local flavors. In fact, During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Middle_Ages" title="European Middle Ages">European Middle Ages</a>, pan-European styles of <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture" title="Gothic architecture">Gothic</a> cathedrals and abbeys emerged while the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_architecture" title="Renaissance architecture">Renaissance</a> favored Classical forms implemented by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Architects" title="Architects">architects</a> known by name. Later, the roles of architects and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Engineers" title="Engineers">engineers</a> became separated. <a href="/wiki/Modern_architecture" title="Modern architecture">Modern architecture</a> began after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> as an <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">avant-garde</a> movement that sought to develop a completely new style appropriate for a new post-war social and economic order focused on meeting the needs of the middle and working classes. Emphasis was put on modern techniques, materials, and simplified geometric forms, paving the way for high-rise superstructures. Many architects became disillusioned with modernism which they perceived as ahistorical and anti-aesthetic, and <a href="/wiki/Postmodern_architecture" title="Postmodern architecture">postmodern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_architecture" title="Contemporary architecture">contemporary architecture</a> developed.
</p><p>Over the years, the field of architectural construction has branched out to include everything from ship design to interior decorating.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span></h2>
<p>Architecture can mean:
</p>
<ul><li>A general term to describe buildings and other physical structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
<li>The art and science of <a href="/wiki/Design" title="Design">designing</a> <a href="/wiki/Building" title="Building">buildings</a> and (some) <a href="/wiki/Nonbuilding_structure" title="Nonbuilding structure">nonbuilding structures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
<li>The style of design and method of construction of buildings and other physical structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-OED-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
<li>A unifying or coherent form or structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></li>
<li>Knowledge of art, science, technology, and humanity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-OED-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
<li>The design activity of the architect,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-OED-8">[8]</a></sup> from the macro-level (<a href="/wiki/Urban_design" title="Urban design">urban design</a>, <a href="/wiki/Landscape_architecture" title="Landscape architecture">landscape architecture</a>) to the micro-level (construction details and furniture). The practice of the <a href="/wiki/Architect" title="Architect">architect</a>, where architecture means offering or rendering professional services in connection with the design and construction of buildings, or built environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theory_of_architecture">Theory of architecture</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Architectural_theory" title="Architectural theory">Architectural theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_architecture" title="Philosophy of architecture">Philosophy of architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yingzao_Fashi_1_desmear.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1064" data-file-width="810" decoding="async" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Yingzao_Fashi_1_desmear.JPG/220px-Yingzao_Fashi_1_desmear.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Yingzao_Fashi_1_desmear.JPG/330px-Yingzao_Fashi_1_desmear.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Yingzao_Fashi_1_desmear.JPG/440px-Yingzao_Fashi_1_desmear.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yingzao_Fashi_1_desmear.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of <a href="/wiki/Bracket_(architecture)" title="Bracket (architecture)">bracket</a> arm clusters containing <a href="/wiki/Cantilever" title="Cantilever">cantilevers</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/Yingzao_Fashi" title="Yingzao Fashi">Yingzao Fashi</a></i>, a text on architecture by <a href="/wiki/Li_Jie_(author)" title="Li Jie (author)">Li Jue</a> (1065–1110)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plan_d%27ex%C3%A9cution_du_second_%C3%A9tage_de_l%27h%C3%B4tel_de_Brionne_(dessin)_De_Cotte_2503c_%E2%80%93_Gallica_2011_(adjusted).jpg"><img alt="Plan d'exécution du second étage de l'hôtel de Brionne (dessin) De Cotte 2503c – Gallica 2011 (adjusted)" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3032" data-file-width="4398" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Plan_d%27ex%C3%A9cution_du_second_%C3%A9tage_de_l%27h%C3%B4tel_de_Brionne_%28dessin%29_De_Cotte_2503c_%E2%80%93_Gallica_2011_%28adjusted%29.jpg/220px-Plan_d%27ex%C3%A9cution_du_second_%C3%A9tage_de_l%27h%C3%B4tel_de_Brionne_%28dessin%29_De_Cotte_2503c_%E2%80%93_Gallica_2011_%28adjusted%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Plan_d%27ex%C3%A9cution_du_second_%C3%A9tage_de_l%27h%C3%B4tel_de_Brionne_%28dessin%29_De_Cotte_2503c_%E2%80%93_Gallica_2011_%28adjusted%29.jpg/330px-Plan_d%27ex%C3%A9cution_du_second_%C3%A9tage_de_l%27h%C3%B4tel_de_Brionne_%28dessin%29_De_Cotte_2503c_%E2%80%93_Gallica_2011_%28adjusted%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Plan_d%27ex%C3%A9cution_du_second_%C3%A9tage_de_l%27h%C3%B4tel_de_Brionne_%28dessin%29_De_Cotte_2503c_%E2%80%93_Gallica_2011_%28adjusted%29.jpg/440px-Plan_d%27ex%C3%A9cution_du_second_%C3%A9tage_de_l%27h%C3%B4tel_de_Brionne_%28dessin%29_De_Cotte_2503c_%E2%80%93_Gallica_2011_%28adjusted%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plan_d%27ex%C3%A9cution_du_second_%C3%A9tage_de_l%27h%C3%B4tel_de_Brionne_(dessin)_De_Cotte_2503c_%E2%80%93_Gallica_2011_(adjusted).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Floor_plan" title="Floor plan">Plan</a> of the second floor (attic storey) of the Hôtel de Brionne in Paris – 1734.</div></div></div>
<p>The philosophy of architecture is a branch of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_art" title="Philosophy of art">philosophy of art</a>, dealing with aesthetic value of architecture, its <a href="/wiki/Semantics" title="Semantics">semantics</a> and in relation with development of <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a>. Many philosophers and theoreticians from <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> to <a href="/wiki/Michel_Foucault" title="Michel Foucault">Michel Foucault</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gilles_Deleuze" title="Gilles Deleuze">Gilles Deleuze</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Venturi" title="Robert Venturi">Robert Venturi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Wittgenstein" title="Ludwig Wittgenstein">Ludwig Wittgenstein</a> have concerned themselves with the nature of architecture and whether or not architecture is distinguished from building.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historic_treatises">Historic treatises</span></h3>
<p>The earliest surviving written work on the subject of architecture is <i><a href="/wiki/De_architectura" title="De architectura">De architectura</a></i> by the Roman architect <a href="/wiki/Vitruvius" title="Vitruvius">Vitruvius</a> in the early 1st century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vitruvius_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vitruvius-12">[12]</a></sup> According to Vitruvius, a good building should satisfy the three principles of <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">firmitas, utilitas, venustas</i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elements_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-elements-14">[14]</a></sup> commonly known by the original translation – <i>firmness, commodity and delight</i>. An equivalent in modern English would be:
</p>
<ul><li>Durability – a building should stand up robustly and remain in good condition</li>
<li>Utility – it should be suitable for the purposes for which it is used</li>
<li>Beauty – it should be aesthetically pleasing</li></ul>
<p>According to Vitruvius, the architect should strive to fulfill each of these three attributes as well as possible. <a href="/wiki/Leon_Battista_Alberti" title="Leon Battista Alberti">Leon Battista Alberti</a>, who elaborates on the ideas of Vitruvius in his treatise, <i><a href="/wiki/De_re_aedificatoria" title="De re aedificatoria">De re aedificatoria</a></i>, saw beauty primarily as a matter of proportion, although ornament also played a part. For Alberti, the rules of proportion were those that governed the idealized human figure, the <a href="/wiki/Golden_ratio" title="Golden ratio">Golden mean</a>. The most important aspect of beauty was, therefore, an inherent part of an object, rather than something applied superficially, and was based on universal, recognizable truths. The notion of style in the arts was not developed until the 16th century, with the writing of <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Vasari" title="Giorgio Vasari">Giorgio Vasari</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> By the 18th century, his <i><a href="/wiki/Lives_of_the_Most_Excellent_Painters,_Sculptors,_and_Architects" title="Lives of the Most Excellent Painters, Sculptors, and Architects">Lives of the Most Excellent Painters, Sculptors, and Architects</a></i> had been translated into Italian, French, Spanish, and English.
</p><p>In the 16th century, Italian Mannerist architect, painter and theorist <a href="/wiki/Sebastiano_Serlio" title="Sebastiano Serlio">Sebastiano Serlio</a> wrote <i>Tutte L'Opere D'Architettura et Prospetiva</i> (<i>Complete Works on Architecture and Perspective</i>). This treatise exerted immense influence throughout Europe, being the first handbook that emphasized the practical rather than the theoretical aspects of architecture, and it was the first to catalog the five orders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 19th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augustus_Welby_Northmore_Pugin" title="Augustus Welby Northmore Pugin">Augustus Welby Northmore Pugin</a> wrote <i>Contrasts</i> (1836) that, as the title suggested, contrasted the modern, industrial world, which he disparaged, with an idealized image of neo-medieval world. <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture" title="Gothic architecture">Gothic architecture</a>, Pugin believed, was the only "true Christian form of architecture."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The 19th-century English art critic, <a href="/wiki/John_Ruskin" title="John Ruskin">John Ruskin</a>, in his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seven_Lamps_of_Architecture" title="Seven Lamps of Architecture">Seven Lamps of Architecture</a></i>, published 1849, was much narrower in his view of what constituted architecture. Architecture was the "art which so disposes and adorns the edifices raised by men … that the sight of them" contributes "to his mental health, power, and pleasure".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruskin_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruskin-18">[18]</a></sup> For Ruskin, the aesthetic was of overriding significance. His work goes on to state that a building is not truly a work of architecture unless it is in some way "adorned". For Ruskin, a well-constructed, well-proportioned, functional building needed string <a href="/wiki/Course_(architecture)" title="Course (architecture)">courses</a> or <a href="/wiki/Rustication_(architecture)" title="Rustication (architecture)">rustication</a>, at the very least.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruskin_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ruskin-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the difference between the ideals of <i>architecture</i> and mere <i><a href="/wiki/Construction" title="Construction">construction</a></i>, the renowned 20th-century architect <a href="/wiki/Le_Corbusier" title="Le Corbusier">Le Corbusier</a> wrote: "You employ stone, wood, and concrete, and with these materials you build houses and palaces: that is construction. Ingenuity is at work. But suddenly you touch my heart, you do me good. I am happy and I say: This is beautiful. That is Architecture".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Le Corbusier's contemporary <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Mies_van_der_Rohe" title="Ludwig Mies van der Rohe">Ludwig Mies van der Rohe</a> said "Architecture starts when you carefully put two bricks together. There it begins."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Congresso_do_Brasil.jpg"><img alt="The view shows a 20th-century building with two identical towers very close to each other rising from a low building which has a dome at one end, and an inverted dome, like a saucer, at the other." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1455" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Congresso_do_Brasil.jpg/220px-Congresso_do_Brasil.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Congresso_do_Brasil.jpg/330px-Congresso_do_Brasil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Congresso_do_Brasil.jpg/440px-Congresso_do_Brasil.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Congresso_do_Brasil.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/National_Congress_of_Brazil" title="National Congress of Brazil">National Congress of Brazil</a>, designed by <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Niemeyer" title="Oscar Niemeyer">Oscar Niemeyer</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_concepts">Modern concepts</span></h3>
<p>The notable 19th-century architect of skyscrapers, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Sullivan" title="Louis Sullivan">Louis Sullivan</a>, promoted an overriding precept to architectural design: "<a href="/wiki/Form_follows_function" title="Form follows function">Form follows function</a>". While the notion that structural and aesthetic considerations should be entirely subject to functionality was met with both popularity and skepticism, it had the effect of introducing the concept of "function" in place of <a href="/wiki/Vitruvius" title="Vitruvius">Vitruvius</a>' "utility". "Function" came to be seen as encompassing all criteria of the use, perception and enjoyment of a <a href="/wiki/Building" title="Building">building</a>, not only practical but also aesthetic, psychological and cultural.
</p><p><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nunzia_Rondanini&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nunzia Rondanini (page does not exist)">Nunzia Rondanini</a> stated, "Through its aesthetic dimension architecture goes beyond the functional aspects that it has in common with other human sciences. Through its own particular way of expressing <a href="/wiki/Architectural_design_values" title="Architectural design values">values</a>, architecture can stimulate and influence social life without presuming that, in and of itself, it will promote social development.... To restrict the meaning of (architectural) formalism to art for art's sake is not only reactionary; it can also be a purposeless quest for perfection or originality which degrades form into a mere instrumentality".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the philosophies that have influenced modern architects and their approach to building design are <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">Rationalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">Empiricism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Structuralism" title="Structuralism">Structuralism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poststructuralism" title="Poststructuralism">Poststructuralism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deconstructivism" title="Deconstructivism">Deconstruction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phenomenology_(architecture)" title="Phenomenology (architecture)">Phenomenology</a>.
</p><p>In the late 20th century a new concept was added to those included in the compass of both structure and function, the consideration of <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a>, hence <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_architecture" title="Sustainable architecture">sustainable architecture</a>. To satisfy the contemporary ethos a building should be constructed in a manner which is environmentally friendly in terms of the production of its materials, its impact upon the natural and built environment of its surrounding area and the demands that it makes upon non-sustainable power sources for heating, cooling, water and waste management, and <a href="/wiki/Lighting" title="Lighting">lighting</a>.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_architecture" title="History of architecture">History of architecture</a></div>

</p>

<p>Building first evolved out of the dynamics between needs (shelter, security, worship, etc.) and means (available <a href="/wiki/Building_material" title="Building material">building materials</a> and attendant skills). As human cultures developed and knowledge began to be formalized through oral traditions and practices, building became a <a href="/wiki/Craft" title="Craft">craft</a>, and "architecture" is the name given to the most highly formalized and respected versions of that craft. It is widely assumed that architectural success was the product of a process of trial and error, with progressively less trial and more replication as the results of the process proved increasingly satisfactory. What is termed <a href="/wiki/Vernacular_architecture" title="Vernacular architecture">vernacular architecture</a> continues to be produced in many parts of the world.
</p>

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 182px"><div style="width: 182px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orkney_Skara_Brae.jpg" title="Excavated dwellings at Skara Brae (Mainland, Orkney, Scotland, UK)"><img alt="Excavated dwellings at Skara Brae (Mainland, Orkney, Scotland, UK)" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Orkney_Skara_Brae.jpg/270px-Orkney_Skara_Brae.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Orkney_Skara_Brae.jpg/406px-Orkney_Skara_Brae.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Orkney_Skara_Brae.jpg/541px-Orkney_Skara_Brae.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Excavated dwellings at <a href="/wiki/Skara_Brae" title="Skara Brae">Skara Brae</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mainland,_Orkney" title="Mainland, Orkney">Mainland, Orkney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, UK)
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Early human settlements were mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rural" title="Rural">rural</a>. Hence, Expending economies resulted in the creation of <a href="/wiki/Urban_area" title="Urban area">urban areas</a> which in some cases grew and evolved very rapidly, such as that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%87atal_H%C3%B6y%C3%BCk" title="Çatal Höyük">Çatal Höyük</a> in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mohenjo-daro" title="Mohenjo-daro">Mohenjo Daro</a> of the Indus Valley Civilization in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.
</p><p>Neolithic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_archaeological_sites" title="List of archaeological sites">settlements and "cities"</a> include <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6bekli_Tepe" title="Göbekli Tepe">Göbekli Tepe</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk" title="Çatalhöyük">Çatalhöyük</a> in Turkey, <a href="/wiki/Jericho" title="Jericho">Jericho</a> in the Levant, <a href="/wiki/Mehrgarh" title="Mehrgarh">Mehrgarh</a> in Pakistan, <a href="/wiki/Knap_of_Howar" title="Knap of Howar">Knap of Howar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skara_Brae" title="Skara Brae">Skara Brae</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orkney_Islands" title="Orkney Islands">Orkney Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Scotland" title="Prehistoric Scotland">Scotland</a>, and the  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cucuteni-Trypillian_culture" title="Cucuteni-Trypillian culture">Cucuteni-Trypillian culture</a> settlements in <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_architecture">Ancient architecture</span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

</p>

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 162px"><div style="width: 162px">

<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Japanese_architecture" title="Japanese architecture">Japanese architecture</a>: <a href="/wiki/H%C5%8Dry%C5%AB-ji" title="Hōryū-ji">Hōryū-ji</a>, a Buddhist temple in <a href="/wiki/Nara_Prefecture" title="Nara Prefecture">Nara Prefecture</a>, Japan, 607 AD
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>In many ancient civilizations such as those of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, architecture and urbanism reflected the constant engagement with the divine and the <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a>, and many ancient cultures resorted to monumentality in architecture to symbolically represent the political power of the ruler or the state itself.
</p><p>The architecture and <a href="/wiki/Urbanism" title="Urbanism">urbanism</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Classical civilizations</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> evolved from civic ideals rather than religious or empirical ones and new building types emerged. As the Architectural "style" developed in the form of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_orders" title="Classical orders">Classical orders</a>. Roman architecture was influenced by Greek architecture as they incorporated many Greek elements into their building practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Texts on architecture have been written since ancient times. These texts provided both general advice and specific formal prescriptions or canons. Some examples of canons are found in the writings of the 1st-century BCE Roman Architect <a href="/wiki/Vitruvius" title="Vitruvius">Vitruvius</a>. Some of the most important early examples of canonic architecture are religious.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asian_architecture">Asian architecture</span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 160.66666666667px"><div style="width: 160.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 158.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beauty_of_khajuraho_temple.jpg" title="Indian architecture: The Kandariya Mahadeva Temple (Khajuraho, Madhya Pradesh, India), circa 1030"><img alt="Indian architecture: The Kandariya Mahadeva Temple (Khajuraho, Madhya Pradesh, India), circa 1030" data-file-height="2730" data-file-width="3601" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Beauty_of_khajuraho_temple.jpg/238px-Beauty_of_khajuraho_temple.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Beauty_of_khajuraho_temple.jpg/356px-Beauty_of_khajuraho_temple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Beauty_of_khajuraho_temple.jpg/475px-Beauty_of_khajuraho_temple.jpg 2x" width="159"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 182px"><div style="width: 182px">

</p>

</div></li>

</p>

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 162px"><div style="width: 162px">

<div class="gallerytext">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The architecture of different parts of <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> developed differently than Europe; and each of Buddhist, Hindu and Sikh architecture had different characteristics. In fact, Unlike <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_architecture" title="Indian architecture">Indian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_architecture" title="Chinese architecture">Chinese</a> architecture which had great influence on the surrounding regions, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_architecture" title="Japanese architecture">Japanese architecture</a> did not. Some Asian architecture showed great regional diversity such as <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_architecture" title="Buddhist architecture">Buddhist architecture</a>, in particular. Moreover, other architectural achievements in Asia is the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_temple_architecture" title="Hindu temple architecture">Hindu temple architecture</a>, which developed from around the 5th century CE, is in theory governed by concepts laid down in the <a href="/wiki/Shastra" title="Shastra">Shastras</a>, and is concerned with expressing the macrocosm and the microcosm.
</p><p>In many Asian countries, pantheistic religion led to architectural forms that were designed specifically to enhance the <a href="/wiki/Natural_landscape" title="Natural landscape">natural landscape</a>. Also, the grandest houses were relatively lightweight structures mainly using wood until recent times, and there are few survivals of great age.  Buddhism was associated with a move to stone and brick religious structures, probably beginning as <a href="/wiki/Rock-cut_architecture" title="Rock-cut architecture">rock-cut architecture</a>, which has often survived very well.
</p><p>Early Asian writings on architecture include the <i>Kao Gong Ji</i> of China from the 7th–5th centuries BCE; the <a href="/wiki/Shilpa_Shastras" title="Shilpa Shastras">Shilpa Shastras</a> of ancient India;  <a href="/wiki/Manjusri_Vasthu_Vidya_Sastra" title="Manjusri Vasthu Vidya Sastra">Manjusri Vasthu Vidya Sastra</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Araniko" title="Araniko">Araniko</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> .
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_architecture">Islamic architecture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic architecture</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 183.33333333333px"><div style="width: 183.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 181.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:C%C3%B3rdoba_(5157827355).jpg" title="Moorish architecture: Grand arches of the Mosque–Cathedral of Córdoba (Córdoba, Spain)"><img alt="Moorish architecture: Grand arches of the Mosque–Cathedral of Córdoba (Córdoba, Spain)" data-file-height="3026" data-file-width="4563" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/C%C3%B3rdoba_%285157827355%29.jpg/272px-C%C3%B3rdoba_%285157827355%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/C%C3%B3rdoba_%285157827355%29.jpg/407px-C%C3%B3rdoba_%285157827355%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/C%C3%B3rdoba_%285157827355%29.jpg/543px-C%C3%B3rdoba_%285157827355%29.jpg 2x" width="182"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 182px"><div style="width: 182px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20180301124354_IMG_4179And6more_Interior_3.jpg" title="Persian architecture: The Jameh Mosque in Isfahan (Iran)"><img alt="Persian architecture: The Jameh Mosque in Isfahan (Iran)" data-file-height="3980" data-file-width="5975" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/20180301124354_IMG_4179And6more_Interior_3.jpg/270px-20180301124354_IMG_4179And6more_Interior_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/20180301124354_IMG_4179And6more_Interior_3.jpg/406px-20180301124354_IMG_4179And6more_Interior_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/20180301124354_IMG_4179And6more_Interior_3.jpg/541px-20180301124354_IMG_4179And6more_Interior_3.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_architecture" title="Persian architecture">Persian architecture</a>: The <a href="/wiki/Jameh_Mosque_of_Isfahan" title="Jameh Mosque of Isfahan">Jameh Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Isfahan" title="Isfahan">Isfahan</a> (<a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>)
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 184.66666666667px"><div style="width: 184.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 182.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taj_Mahal,_Agra,_India_edit2.jpg" title="Mughal architecture: The Taj Mahal in Agra (India)"><img alt="Mughal architecture: The Taj Mahal in Agra (India)" data-file-height="2525" data-file-width="3840" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Taj_Mahal%2C_Agra%2C_India_edit2.jpg/274px-Taj_Mahal%2C_Agra%2C_India_edit2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Taj_Mahal%2C_Agra%2C_India_edit2.jpg/411px-Taj_Mahal%2C_Agra%2C_India_edit2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Taj_Mahal%2C_Agra%2C_India_edit2.jpg/548px-Taj_Mahal%2C_Agra%2C_India_edit2.jpg 2x" width="183"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 182px"><div style="width: 182px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Selimiye_Mosque,_Dome.jpg" title="Ottoman architecture: The interior side view of the main dome of the Selimiye Mosque in Edirne (Turkey)"><img alt="Ottoman architecture: The interior side view of the main dome of the Selimiye Mosque in Edirne (Turkey)" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Selimiye_Mosque%2C_Dome.jpg/270px-Selimiye_Mosque%2C_Dome.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Selimiye_Mosque%2C_Dome.jpg/405px-Selimiye_Mosque%2C_Dome.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Selimiye_Mosque%2C_Dome.jpg/540px-Selimiye_Mosque%2C_Dome.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Ottoman_architecture" title="Ottoman architecture">Ottoman architecture</a>: The interior side view of the main dome of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selimiye_Mosque_(Edirne)" title="Selimiye Mosque (Edirne)">Selimiye Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Edirne" title="Edirne">Edirne</a> (<a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>)
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Islamic architecture began in the 7th century <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>, incorporating architectural forms from the ancient <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a>, but also developing features to suit the religious and social needs of the society. Examples can be found throughout the Middle East, Turkey, North Africa, the Indian Sub-continent  and in parts of Europe, such as Spain, Albania, and the Balkan States, as the result of the expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metmuseum.org-ottoman-europe_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-metmuseum.org-ottoman-europe-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brill.com-book-Lory_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-brill.com-book-Lory-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Middle_Ages">European Middle Ages</span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 100px"><div style="width: 100px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 98px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Basilica_di_Santa_Maria_Maggiore_abside_a_Roma.jpg" title="Byzantine architecture: Apse of Santa Maria Maggiore (Rome), decorated in the 5th century with this glamorous mosaic"><img alt="Byzantine architecture: Apse of Santa Maria Maggiore (Rome), decorated in the 5th century with this glamorous mosaic" data-file-height="7934" data-file-width="6500" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Basilica_di_Santa_Maria_Maggiore_abside_a_Roma.jpg/147px-Basilica_di_Santa_Maria_Maggiore_abside_a_Roma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Basilica_di_Santa_Maria_Maggiore_abside_a_Roma.jpg/221px-Basilica_di_Santa_Maria_Maggiore_abside_a_Roma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Basilica_di_Santa_Maria_Maggiore_abside_a_Roma.jpg/295px-Basilica_di_Santa_Maria_Maggiore_abside_a_Roma.jpg 2x" width="98"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 182px"><div style="width: 182px">

</p>

</p>

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 203.33333333333px"><div style="width: 203.33333333333px">

<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Br%C3%A2ncovenesc_architecture" title="Brâncovenesc architecture">Brâncovenesc architecture</a>: The <a href="/wiki/Stavropoleos_Monastery" title="Stavropoleos Monastery">Stavropoleos Church</a> (downtown <a href="/wiki/Bucharest" title="Bucharest">Bucharest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>), with elaborate paintings on the façade, 1724
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval" title="Medieval">Medieval</a> period, <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guilds</a> were formed by craftsmen to organize their trades and written contracts have survived, particularly in relation to ecclesiastical buildings. The role of architect was usually one with that of master mason, or <i>Magister lathomorum</i> as they are sometimes described in contemporary documents.
</p><p>The major architectural undertakings were the buildings of abbeys and <a href="/wiki/Cathedral" title="Cathedral">cathedrals</a>. From about 900 CE onward, the movements of both clerics and tradesmen carried architectural knowledge across Europe, resulting in the pan-European styles <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture" title="Gothic architecture">Gothic</a>.
</p><p>Also, a significant part of the Middle Ages architectural heritage is numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fortifications" title="Fortifications">fortifications</a> across the continent. From the Balkans to Spain, and from Malta to Estonia, these buildings represent an important part of European heritage.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Renaissance_and_the_architect">Renaissance and the architect</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_architecture" title="Renaissance architecture">Renaissance architecture</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 162px"><div style="width: 162px">

</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 182px"><div style="width: 182px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Schloss_Chenonceau.JPG" title="The Château de Chenonceau (France), by Philibert de l'Orme, 1576"><img alt="The Château de Chenonceau (France), by Philibert de l'Orme, 1576" data-file-height="1993" data-file-width="2990" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Schloss_Chenonceau.JPG/270px-Schloss_Chenonceau.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Schloss_Chenonceau.JPG/405px-Schloss_Chenonceau.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Schloss_Chenonceau.JPG/540px-Schloss_Chenonceau.JPG 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Chenonceau" title="Château de Chenonceau">Château de Chenonceau</a> (France), by <a href="/wiki/Philibert_de_l%27Orme" title="Philibert de l'Orme">Philibert de l'Orme</a>, 1576
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> Europe, from about 1400 onwards, there was a revival of Classical learning accompanied by the development of <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">Renaissance humanism</a>, which placed greater emphasis on the role of the individual in society than had been the case during the Medieval period. Buildings were ascribed to specific architects – <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Brunelleschi" title="Filippo Brunelleschi">Brunelleschi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leone_Battista_Alberti" title="Leone Battista Alberti">Alberti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palladio" title="Palladio">Palladio</a> – and the cult of the individual had begun. There was still no dividing line between <a href="/wiki/Artist" title="Artist">artist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Architect" title="Architect">architect</a> and <a href="/wiki/Engineer" title="Engineer">engineer</a>, or any of the related vocations, and the appellation was often one of regional preference.
</p><p>A revival of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_architecture" title="Classical architecture">Classical style</a> in architecture was accompanied by a burgeoning of science and engineering, which affected the proportions and structure of buildings. At this stage, it was still possible for an artist to design a bridge as the level of structural calculations involved was within the scope of the generalist.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_and_the_industrial_age">Early modern and the industrial age</span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 174px"><div style="width: 174px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 172px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Maisons-Laffitte_001.jpg" title="Baroque architecture: The Château de Maisons (France), by François Mansart, 1630–1651"><img alt="Baroque architecture: The Château de Maisons (France), by François Mansart, 1630–1651" data-file-height="2775" data-file-width="3970" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Maisons-Laffitte_001.jpg/258px-Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Maisons-Laffitte_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Maisons-Laffitte_001.jpg/386px-Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Maisons-Laffitte_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Maisons-Laffitte_001.jpg/515px-Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Maisons-Laffitte_001.jpg 2x" width="172"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture" title="Baroque architecture">Baroque architecture</a>: The <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Maisons" title="Château de Maisons">Château de Maisons</a> (France), by <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Mansart" title="François Mansart">François Mansart</a>, 1630–1651
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 182px"><div style="width: 182px">

<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 122px"><div style="width: 122px">

<div class="gallerytext">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The emerging knowledge in scientific fields and the rise of new materials and technology, architecture and <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a> began to separate, and the architect began to concentrate on <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetics</a> and the humanist aspects, often at the expense of technical aspects of building design. There was also the rise of the "gentleman architect" who usually dealt with wealthy clients and concentrated predominantly on visual qualities derived usually from historical prototypes, typified by the many country houses of Great Britain that were created in the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival_architecture" title="Gothic Revival architecture">Neo Gothic</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_baronial" title="Scottish baronial">Scottish baronial</a> styles.
Formal architectural training in the 19th century, for example at <a href="/wiki/%C3%89cole_des_Beaux-Arts" title="École des Beaux-Arts">École des Beaux-Arts</a> in France, gave much emphasis to the production of beautiful drawings and little to context and feasibility.
</p><p>Meanwhile, the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> laid open the door for mass production and consumption. Aesthetics became a criterion for the middle class as ornamented products, once within the province of expensive craftsmanship, became cheaper under machine production.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Vernacular_architecture" title="Vernacular architecture">Vernacular architecture</a> became increasingly ornamental. Housebuilders could use current architectural design in their work by combining features found in pattern books and architectural journals.
</p>

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 122px"><div style="width: 122px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 120px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Overzicht_voorgevel_-_Heerlen_-_20349660_-_RCE.jpg" title="International Style: The Glaspaleis (Heerlen, the Netherlands), 1934–1935, by Frits Peutz and Philip Johnson"><img alt="International Style: The Glaspaleis (Heerlen, the Netherlands), 1934–1935, by Frits Peutz and Philip Johnson" data-file-height="2880" data-file-width="2880" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Overzicht_voorgevel_-_Heerlen_-_20349660_-_RCE.jpg/180px-Overzicht_voorgevel_-_Heerlen_-_20349660_-_RCE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Overzicht_voorgevel_-_Heerlen_-_20349660_-_RCE.jpg/270px-Overzicht_voorgevel_-_Heerlen_-_20349660_-_RCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Overzicht_voorgevel_-_Heerlen_-_20349660_-_RCE.jpg/360px-Overzicht_voorgevel_-_Heerlen_-_20349660_-_RCE.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/International_Style_(architecture)" title="International Style (architecture)">International Style</a>: The <a href="/wiki/Glaspaleis" title="Glaspaleis">Glaspaleis</a> (<a href="/wiki/Heerlen" title="Heerlen">Heerlen</a>, the Netherlands), 1934–1935, by <a href="/wiki/Frits_Peutz" title="Frits Peutz">Frits Peutz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philip_Johnson" title="Philip Johnson">Philip Johnson</a>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Around the beginning of the 20th century, general dissatisfaction with the emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Revivalism_(architecture)" title="Revivalism (architecture)">revivalist architecture</a> and elaborate decoration gave rise to many new lines of thought that served as precursors to Modern architecture. Notable among these is the <a href="/wiki/Deutscher_Werkbund" title="Deutscher Werkbund">Deutscher Werkbund</a>, formed in 1907 to produce better quality machine-made objects. The rise of the profession of industrial design is usually placed here. Following this lead, the <a href="/wiki/Bauhaus" title="Bauhaus">Bauhaus</a> school, founded in <a href="/wiki/Weimar" title="Weimar">Weimar</a>, Germany in 1919, redefined the architectural bounds prior set throughout history, viewing the creation of a building as the ultimate synthesis—the apex—of art, craft, and technology.
</p><p>When <a href="/wiki/Modern_architecture" title="Modern architecture">modern architecture</a> was first practiced, it was an <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">avant-garde</a> movement with moral, philosophical, and aesthetic underpinnings. Immediately after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, pioneering modernist architects sought to develop a completely new style appropriate for a new post-war social and economic order, focused on meeting the needs of the middle and working classes. They rejected the architectural practice of the academic refinement of historical styles which served the rapidly declining aristocratic order. The approach of the Modernist architects was to reduce buildings to pure forms, removing historical references and ornament in favor of functional details. Buildings displayed their functional and structural elements, exposing steel beams and concrete surfaces instead of hiding them behind decorative forms. Architects such as <a href="/wiki/Frank_Lloyd_Wright" title="Frank Lloyd Wright">Frank Lloyd Wright</a> developed <a href="/wiki/Organic_architecture" title="Organic architecture">organic architecture</a>, in which the form was defined by its environment and purpose, with an aim to promote harmony between human habitation and the natural world with prime examples being <a href="/wiki/Robie_House" title="Robie House">Robie House</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fallingwater" title="Fallingwater">Fallingwater</a>.
</p><p>Architects such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mies_van_der_Rohe" title="Mies van der Rohe">Mies van der Rohe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Johnson" title="Philip Johnson">Philip Johnson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Breuer" title="Marcel Breuer">Marcel Breuer</a> worked to create beauty based on the inherent qualities of building materials and modern construction techniques, trading traditional historic forms for simplified geometric forms, celebrating the new means and methods made possible by the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, including steel-frame construction, which gave birth to high-rise superstructures. <a href="/wiki/Fazlur_Rahman_Khan" title="Fazlur Rahman Khan">Fazlur Rahman Khan</a>'s development of the <a href="/wiki/Tube_(structure)" title="Tube (structure)">tube structure</a> was a technological break-through in building ever higher.  By mid-century, Modernism had morphed into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_style_(architecture)" title="International style (architecture)">International Style</a>, an aesthetic epitomized in many ways by the Twin Towers of New York's <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(1973%E2%80%932001)" title="World Trade Center (1973–2001)">World Trade Center</a> designed by <a href="/wiki/Minoru_Yamasaki" title="Minoru Yamasaki">Minoru Yamasaki</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postmodernism">Postmodernism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Postmodern_architecture" title="Postmodern architecture">Postmodern architecture</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 206.66666666667px"><div style="width: 206.66666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 204.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PiazzaDItalia1990.jpg" title="Piazza d'Italia (New Orleans, USA), 1978, by Charles Moore"><img alt="Piazza d'Italia (New Orleans, USA), 1978, by Charles Moore" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/PiazzaDItalia1990.jpg/307px-PiazzaDItalia1990.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/PiazzaDItalia1990.jpg/460px-PiazzaDItalia1990.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/PiazzaDItalia1990.jpg/614px-PiazzaDItalia1990.jpg 2x" width="205"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Piazza_d%27Italia_(New_Orleans)" title="Piazza d'Italia (New Orleans)">Piazza d'Italia</a> (<a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>, USA), 1978, by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Moore_(architect)" title="Charles Moore (architect)">Charles Moore</a>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 242px"><div style="width: 242px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 240px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Walt_Disney_Company_office.jpg" title="Team Disney Building (Los Angeles, USA), 1990, by Michael Graves"><img alt="Team Disney Building (Los Angeles, USA), 1990, by Michael Graves" data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/The_Walt_Disney_Company_office.jpg/360px-The_Walt_Disney_Company_office.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/The_Walt_Disney_Company_office.jpg/540px-The_Walt_Disney_Company_office.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/The_Walt_Disney_Company_office.jpg/720px-The_Walt_Disney_Company_office.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Team_Disney" title="Team Disney">Team Disney</a> Building (<a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>, USA), 1990, by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Graves" title="Michael Graves">Michael Graves</a>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 92px"><div style="width: 92px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 90px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cambridge_University_Judge_Business_School_interior.jpg" title="Multicolour interior of the Cambridge Judge Business School (Cambridge, the UK), 1995, by John Outram"><img alt="Multicolour interior of the Cambridge Judge Business School (Cambridge, the UK), 1995, by John Outram" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Cambridge_University_Judge_Business_School_interior.jpg/135px-Cambridge_University_Judge_Business_School_interior.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Cambridge_University_Judge_Business_School_interior.jpg/202px-Cambridge_University_Judge_Business_School_interior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Cambridge_University_Judge_Business_School_interior.jpg/270px-Cambridge_University_Judge_Business_School_interior.jpg 2x" width="90"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 98px"><div style="width: 98px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 96px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Case_danzanti.jpg" title="The Dancing House (Prague, Czech Republic), 1996, by Vlado Milunić and Frank Gehry"><img alt="The Dancing House (Prague, Czech Republic), 1996, by Vlado Milunić and Frank Gehry" data-file-height="1501" data-file-width="1199" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Case_danzanti.jpg/144px-Case_danzanti.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Case_danzanti.jpg/216px-Case_danzanti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Case_danzanti.jpg/287px-Case_danzanti.jpg 2x" width="96"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dancing_House" title="Dancing House">Dancing House</a> (<a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a>, <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>), 1996, by <a href="/wiki/Vlado_Miluni%C4%87" title="Vlado Milunić">Vlado Milunić</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Gehry" title="Frank Gehry">Frank Gehry</a>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<p>Many architects resisted <a href="/wiki/Modernism" title="Modernism">modernism</a>, finding it devoid of the decorative richness of historical styles. As the first generation of modernists began to die after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the second generation of architects including <a href="/wiki/Paul_Rudolph_(architect)" title="Paul Rudolph (architect)">Paul Rudolph</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Breuer" title="Marcel Breuer">Marcel Breuer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eero_Saarinen" title="Eero Saarinen">Eero Saarinen</a> tried to expand the aesthetics of modernism with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brutalism" title="Brutalism">Brutalism</a>, buildings with expressive sculpture façades made of unfinished concrete. But an even  younger postwar generation critiqued modernism and Brutalism for being too austere, standardized, monotone, and not taking into account the richness of human experience offered in historical buildings across time and in different places and cultures.
</p><p>One such reaction to the cold aesthetic of modernism and Brutalism is the school of <a href="/wiki/Metaphoric_architecture" title="Metaphoric architecture">metaphoric architecture</a>, which includes such things as <a href="/wiki/Biomorphism" title="Biomorphism">bio morphism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zoomorphic_architecture" title="Zoomorphic architecture">zoomorphic architecture</a>, both using nature as the primary source of inspiration and design.  While it is considered by some to be merely an aspect of <a href="/wiki/Postmodernism" title="Postmodernism">postmodernism</a>, others consider it to be a school in its own right and a later development of <a href="/wiki/Expressionist_architecture" title="Expressionist architecture">expressionist architecture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metaphor_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-metaphor-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beginning in the late 1950s and 1960s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Architectural_phenomenology" title="Architectural phenomenology">architectural phenomenology</a> emerged as an important movement in the early reaction against modernism, with architects like <a href="/wiki/Charles_Moore_(architect)" title="Charles Moore (architect)">Charles Moore</a> in the United States, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Norberg-Schulz" title="Christian Norberg-Schulz">Christian Norberg-Schulz</a> in Norway, and <a href="/wiki/Ernesto_Nathan_Rogers" title="Ernesto Nathan Rogers">Ernesto Nathan Rogers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vittorio_Gregotti" title="Vittorio Gregotti">Vittorio Gregotti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michele_Valori" title="Michele Valori">Michele Valori</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Zevi" title="Bruno Zevi">Bruno Zevi</a> in Italy, who collectively popularized an interest in a new contemporary architecture aimed at expanding human experience using historical buildings as models and precedents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Postmodernism produced a style that combined contemporary building technology and cheap materials, with the aesthetics of older pre-modern and non-modern styles, from high classical architecture to popular or vernacular regional building styles. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Venturi" title="Robert Venturi">Robert Venturi</a> famously defined postmodern architecture as a "decorated shed" (an ordinary building which is functionally designed inside and embellished on the outside) and upheld it against modernist and brutalist "ducks" (buildings with unnecessarily expressive tectonic forms).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture_today">Architecture today</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_architecture" title="Contemporary architecture">Contemporary architecture</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 162px"><div style="width: 162px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 160px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dallas_Meadows_Museum_1.jpg" title="The Meadows Museum (Dallas, Texas, USA), 2001, by HBRA architects"><img alt="The Meadows Museum (Dallas, Texas, USA), 2001, by HBRA architects" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Dallas_Meadows_Museum_1.jpg/240px-Dallas_Meadows_Museum_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Dallas_Meadows_Museum_1.jpg/360px-Dallas_Meadows_Museum_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Dallas_Meadows_Museum_1.jpg/480px-Dallas_Meadows_Museum_1.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Meadows_Museum" title="Meadows Museum">Meadows Museum</a> (<a href="/wiki/Dallas" title="Dallas">Dallas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, USA), 2001, by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_H._Beeby" title="Thomas H. Beeby">HBRA architects</a>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 210px"><div style="width: 210px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 208px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beijing_national_stadium.jpg" title="The Beijing National Stadium (Beijing, China), 2003–2007, by Herzog &amp; de Meuron"><img alt="The Beijing National Stadium (Beijing, China), 2003–2007, by Herzog &amp; de Meuron" data-file-height="1019" data-file-width="1762" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Beijing_national_stadium.jpg/312px-Beijing_national_stadium.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Beijing_national_stadium.jpg/467px-Beijing_national_stadium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Beijing_national_stadium.jpg/623px-Beijing_national_stadium.jpg 2x" width="208"/></a></div></div>
